<p><a href="/wiki/Lager" title="Lager">Lagers</a> are stored at near freezing temperatures for 1–6 months while still on the yeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The process of storing, or conditioning, or maturing, or aging a beer at a low temperature for a long period is called "lagering", and while it is associated with lagers, the process may also be done with ales, with the same result – that of cleaning up various chemicals, acids and compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Secondary fermentation<span id="Brewing.23Secondary_fermentation"></span></dt>
</dl>
<p>During secondary fermentation, most of the remaining yeast will settle to the bottom of the second fermenter, yielding a less hazy product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt><span id="Bottle_conditioning"></span>Bottle fermentation</dt>
</dl>
<p>Some beers undergo an additional fermentation in the bottle giving natural carbonation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> This may be a second or third fermentation. They are bottled with a viable yeast population in suspension. If there is no residual fermentable sugar left, sugar or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wort_(brewing)" title="Wort (brewing)">wort</a> or both may be added in a process known as priming. The resulting fermentation generates <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> that is trapped in the bottle, remaining in solution and providing natural carbonation. Bottle-conditioned beers may be either filled unfiltered direct from the fermentation or conditioning tank, or filtered and then reseeded with yeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Cask conditioning</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cask_ale" title="Cask ale">Cask ale</a></div>
<p>Cask ale or cask-conditioned beer is <a href="/wiki/Filtration" title="Filtration">unfiltered</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">unpasteurised</a> beer that is conditioned (including secondary fermentation) and served from a cask, either pumped up from a cellar via a <a href="/wiki/Beer_engine" title="Beer engine">beer engine</a> (hand pump), or from a tap by gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Sometimes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cask_breather" title="Cask breather">cask breather</a> is used to keep the beer fresh by allowing carbon dioxide to replace oxygen as the beer is drawn off the cask.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_ale" title="Real ale">real ale</a>" as used by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Real_Ale" title="Campaign for Real Ale">Campaign for Real Ale</a> (CAMRA) refers to beer "served without the use of <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">extraneous carbon dioxide</a>", which would disallow the use of a cask breather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAMRA_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAMRA-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Filtering">Filtering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brewing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Filtering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Brewing&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(April 2012)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Filtered_beer" title="Filtered beer">Filtered beer</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="907" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg/220px-DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg/330px-DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg/440px-DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DE_and_yeast_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A mixture of <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> after filtering</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Filtering the beer stabilizes the flavour, and gives beer its polished shine and brilliance. Not all beer is filtered. When tax determination is required by local laws, it is typically done at this stage in a calibrated tank. There are several forms of filters, they may be in the form of sheets or "candles", or they may be a fine powder such as <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a>, also called kieselguhr. The powder is added to the beer and recirculated past screens to form a filtration bed.</p>
<p>Filters range from rough filters that remove much of the yeast and any solids (e.g., hops, grain particles) left in the beer, to filters tight enough to strain colour and body from the beer. Filtration ratings are divided into rough, fine, and sterile. Rough filtration leaves some cloudiness in the beer, but it is noticeably clearer than unfiltered beer. Fine filtration removes almost all cloudiness. Sterile filtration removes almost all microorganisms.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Sheet (pad) filters</dt>
</dl>
<p>These filters use sheets that allow only particles smaller than a given size to pass through. The sheets are placed into a filtering frame, sanitized (with boiling water, for example) and then used to filter the beer. The sheets can be flushed if the filter becomes blocked. The sheets are usually disposable and are replaced between filtration sessions. Often the sheets contain powdered filtration media to aid in filtration.</p>
<p>Pre-made filters have two sides. One with loose holes, and the other with tight holes. Flow goes from the side with loose holes to the side with the tight holes, with the intent that large particles get stuck in the large holes while leaving enough room around the particles and filter medium for smaller particles to go through and get stuck in tighter holes.</p>
<p>Sheets are sold in nominal ratings, and typically 90% of particles larger than the nominal rating are caught by the sheet.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Kieselguhr filters</dt>
</dl>
<p>Filters that use a powder medium are considerably more complicated to operate, but can filter much more beer before regeneration. Common media include <a href="/wiki/Diatomaceous_earth" title="Diatomaceous earth">diatomaceous earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perlite" title="Perlite">perlite</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Packaging">Packaging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brewing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Packaging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beer_bottle" title="Beer bottle">Beer bottle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beverage_can" title="Beverage can">Beverage can</a>, <a href="/wiki/Widget_(beer)" title="Widget (beer)">Widget (beer)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Draught_beer" title="Draught beer">Draught beer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cask_ale" title="Cask ale">Cask ale</a></div>
<p>Packaging is putting the beer into the containers in which it will leave the brewery. Typically, this means putting the beer into bottles, aluminium cans, <a href="/wiki/Keg" title="Keg">kegs</a>, or casks, but it may include putting the beer into bulk tanks for high-volume customers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By-products">By-products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brewing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: By-products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span id="Brewer.27s_spent_grain"></span></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spent_grain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Spent_grain.jpg/220px-Spent_grain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Spent_grain.jpg/330px-Spent_grain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Spent_grain.jpg/440px-Spent_grain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spent_grain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spent grain, a brewing by-product</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Brewing by-products are "spent grain" and the sediment (or "<a href="/wiki/Sediment#Dregs" title="Sediment">dregs</a>") from the filtration process which may be dried and resold as "brewers dried yeast" for poultry feed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> or made into <a href="/wiki/Yeast_extract" title="Yeast extract">yeast extract</a> which is used in brands such as <a href="/wiki/Vegemite" title="Vegemite">Vegemite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marmite" title="Marmite">Marmite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The process of turning the yeast sediment into edible yeast extract was discovered by German scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justus_Liebig" title="Justus Liebig">Justus Liebig</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brewer's spent grain (also called spent grain, brewer's grain or draff) is the main by-product of the brewing process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> it consists of the residue of malt and grain which remains in the mash-kettle after the mashing and lautering process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beldman_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-beldman-142">[142]</a></sup> It consists primarily of grain husks, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericarp" title="Pericarp">pericarp</a>, and fragments of <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forssell_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-forssell-143">[143]</a></sup> As it mainly consists of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forssell_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-forssell-143">[143]</a></sup> and is readily consumed by animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-144">[144]</a></sup> spent grain is used in <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">animal feed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-144">[144]</a></sup> Spent grains can also be used as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>, whole grains in bread,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reinhart_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reinhart-145">[145]</a></sup> as well as in the production of <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. Spent grain is also an ideal medium for growing <a href="/wiki/Edible_mushroom" title="Edible mushroom">mushrooms</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Shiitake" title="Shiitake">shiitake</a>, and already some breweries are either growing their own mushrooms or supplying spent grain to mushroom farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Spent grains can be used in the production of red bricks, to improve the open porosity and reduce thermal conductivity of the ceramic mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brewing_industry">Brewing industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brewing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Brewing industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The brewing industry is a global business, consisting of several dominant <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational companies</a> and many thousands of smaller producers known as <a href="/wiki/Microbrewery" title="Microbrewery">microbreweries</a> or regional breweries depending on size and region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-market_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-market-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> More than 133 billion liters (3.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> U.S. gallons; 2.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> imperial gallons) are sold per year—producing total global revenues of $294.5 billion (£147.7 billion) as of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-researchandmarkets1_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-researchandmarkets1-149">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/SABMiller" title="SABMiller">SABMiller</a> became the largest brewing company in the world when it acquired Royal Grolsch, brewer of Dutch premium beer brand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grolsch" title="Grolsch">Grolsch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/InBev" title="InBev">InBev</a> was the second-largest beer-producing company in the world and <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch" title="Anheuser-Busch">Anheuser-Busch</a> held the third spot, but after the acquisition of Anheuser-Busch by InBev, the new <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch_InBev" title="Anheuser-Busch InBev">Anheuser-Busch InBev</a> company is currently the largest brewer in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Homebrewing" title="Homebrewing">Brewing at home</a> is subject to regulation and prohibition in many countries. Restrictions on homebrewing were lifted in the UK in 1963,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukrepeal_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ukrepeal-152">[152]</a></sup> Australia followed suit in 1972,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ausrepeal_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-ausrepeal-153">[153]</a></sup> and the USA in 1978, though individual states were allowed to pass their own laws limiting production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
